Rather, the Bose 700 are in a newly minted \"ultra-premium\" category, while the QC35 II exist alongside them as the standard premium option. It was around the release of the Bose 700 in 2019 that the company reduced the retail price of the QC35 II from $350 to $300, and now they are even cheaper for Prime Day.</p><div id=\"ProductCard_5d236c62a17d6c48f04e3525\" style=\"position:relative; display:block; width: 100%\"><iframe src=\"https://produktor.businessinsider.com/productCardService?id=5d236c62a17d6c48f04e3525&type=medium&live=true\" width=\"100%\" height=\"150%\" frameborder=\"0\" allowfullscreen=\"\" style=\"position:absolute; top:0; left: 0\"></iframe></div><h2>Bose's QC35 IIs are still excellent headphones — especially for this deal price</h2><p>The Bose QC series has been mostly unrivalled since it was first released, and it's usually the first set of headphones people think of when they're looking for noise-cancelling tech and great audio quality.</p><p>With the <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B0756GB78C?tag=biip_101320_amazon-prime-day-2020-20&linkCode=ogi&th=1&psc=1=1&#38;psc=1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"nofollow noopener sponsored\">QC35 II</a> headphones, you're getting deep, rich sound with a healthy dose of bass and balance of mids and highs. They're also incredibly comfortable headphones with plush and soft ear pads and plenty of room for your ears. Their noise cancelling is top-notch and only bested by Bose's own 700 headphones, and perhaps Sony's most recent $350 WH-1000XM4 headphones. And as for battery life, Bose's 20-hour claim seems pretty spot-on.&#160;</p><p>You can check out the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/bose-700-wireless-noise-cancelling-headphones-review-nearly-perfect-2019-7\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Bose 700 review here</a>&#160;and the <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/sony-wh-1000xm4-wireless-noise-cancelling-headphones-review\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">Sony WH-1000XM4 review here</a>.</p><p>While the QC35 II are excellent, they're not entirely perfect. For one, I expected better build quality for a pair of headphones that used to cost $350. It's not bad, but they're made of light and hollow plastic that can easily scuff over time. But that's inconsequential to their primary function of being comfortable wireless noise-cancelling headphones.&#160;</p><p>Something else to note is that the <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/dp/B0756GB78C?tag=biip_101320_amazon-prime-day-2020-20&linkCode=ogi&th=1&psc=1=1&#38;psc=1\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"nofollow noopener sponsored\">QC35 II</a> use the old microUSB standard for charging. It's only an issue if you've been ditching microUSB by upgrading your devices and accessories to those that use the newer USB-C standard. It might simply mean that you have to bring along a microUSB cable alongside USB-C cables.&#160;</p><p>There's no such thing as the perfect device, and this discount more than makes up for any complaints.&#160;</p><h2>For more headphone and speaker recommendations, check out our various buying guides:</h2><ul><li><strong><a 20:57:00","updatedTime":"2020-10-13 20:57:00","title":"COVID-19 can survive on phone screens for 28 days in the dark, study suggests","link":"https://www.businessinsider.com/covid-19-can-survive-on-common-surfaces-for-28-days-2020-10","description":"<figure>\n\t<img src=\"https://i.insider.com/5e6282b2fee23d28881eb535?format=jpeg\" alt=\"medical coronavirus virus flu sick cold hygiene hand sanitizer clorox hands wash phone cox 1\" height=\"1667\" width=\"2500\" charset=\"\">\n<figcaption>\n<p class=\"copyright\">Crystal Cox/Business Insider</p>\n</figcaption>\n</figure><ul class=\"summary-list\">\n<li>Research from Australia's national science agency suggests that the COVID-19 virus can survive on smooth surfaces for 28 days at room temperature.</li>\n<li>The study tested the virus on glass mobile phone screens, plastic and paper banknotes, and stainless steel.</li>\n<li>Researchers kept these surfaces in the dark during the study. UV light has been shown to kill COVID-19.</li>\n<li>Previous studies have suggested the virus lingers on these surfaces for seven days or less.</li>\n<li><a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/?hprecirc-bullet\">Visit Business Insider's homepage for more stories</a>.</li>\n</ul><p>The COVID-19 virus can survive on phone screens for 28 days under laboratory conditions, longer than previously thought, new research from the Australian government's science agency has found.&#160;</p><p>Researchers tested the virus on smooth surfaces such as glass phone screens and paper banknotes. They kept them in the dark at room temperature, around 20 degrees Celsius (68 degrees Fahrenheit).</p><p>They found the virus could survive for just under a month in these conditions.</p><p>UV light has been shown to kill COVID-19 in previous research.</p><p>The findings of the study, conducted by the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Research Organisation, were <a href=\"https://virologyj.biomedcentral.com/articles/10.1186/s12985-020-01418-7\">published</a> on October 7&#160;in Virology Journal.</p><p><a href=\"https://www.thelancet.com/journals/lanmic/article/PIIS2666-5247(20)30003-3/fulltext\">Previous research</a> suggested that SARS-Cov-2 survives on glass and bank notes for about two to three days, and lingers on plastic and stainless steel for six days at most. One study, published in <a href=\"https://www.thelancet.com/action/showPdf?pii=S2666-5247%2820%2930003-3\">The Lancet,</a> suggested the virus suggests the virus can survive for seven days for stainless steel.</p><p>In the latest study, the virus did not survive on porous materials such as cloth past a 14-day period. In comparison, the flu virus can survive on these surfaces in the same conditions for 17 days.</p><p>The study also found that increasing the temperature reduced the survivability of the virus to as little as 24 hours at 40 degrees Celsius (104 degrees Fahrenheit) on some surfaces.</p><p>The <a href=\"https://www.cdc.gov/coronavirus/2019-ncov/prevent-getting-sick/how-covid-spreads.html\">US Centers for Disease Control</a> says that a person can contract COVID-19 by touching a surface and then touching their mouth, eyes, or nose, but this is believed to be much less common that the virus spreading through close contact between people.</p><p>COVID-19 has killed more than <a href=\"https://www.businessinsider.com/coronavirus-global-death-toll-may-be-higher-1-million-2020-9\">1 million people</a> and infected more than 37.4 million. Cases are now on the rise at an increasing race, especially in Europe and South America.</p><div class=\"read-original\">Read the original article on <a
